aLaboratory of Agricultural Information Intelligent Sensing, School of Biosystems Engineering and Food Science, Zhejiang University, Hangzhou, Zhejiang 310058, China. E-mail: jfping@zju.edu.cn

Received 
      23rd February 2020
    First published on 27th May 2020AbstractFrom environmental monitoring to point-of-care biofluid analysis, rapid ion determination requires robust analytical tools. In recent years, driven by the development of materials science and processing technology, solid-contact ion-selective electrodes (SC-ISEs) with high-performance functional materials and creative structures have shown great potential for routine and portable ion detection. In particular, the introduction of nanomaterials as ion-to-electron transducers and the adoption of different performance enhancement strategies have significantly promoted the development of SC-ISEs. Besides, with the increasing miniaturization, flexibility, and dependability of SC-ISEs, this field has gradually begun to evolve from conventional potentiometric ion sensing to integrated sensing systems with broader application scenarios. This comprehensive review covers pioneering research on functional materials and state-of-the-art technologies for the construction of SC-ISEs, with an emphasis on new development trends and applications.
Yuzhou Shao
Yuzhou Shao received his BS (2018) degree from the School of Biosystems Engineering and Food Science at Zhejiang University. He is currently studying for his MS degree under the supervision of Prof. Jianfeng Ping. His current research focuses on nanomaterial-based battery-free wireless biosensing devices for agriculture.
Yibin Ying
Yibin Ying received his PhD degree in agricultural engineering. Now he is a Qiushi Distinguished Professor of Zhejiang University and the President of Zhejiang A&F University. He leads the Intelligent Bioindustrial Equipment Innovation Team at Zhejiang University. His research interests focus on the development of technology and intelligent equipment for agricultural products.
Jianfeng Ping
Jianfeng Ping is a ZJU-100 Young Professor at Zhejiang University. He obtained his BS degree from the Zhejiang University of Technology and his PhD degree from Zhejiang University. His current research interests focus on battery-free, flexible, stretchable, wireless, green, biodegradable, breathable, and wearable sensing devices for agriculture.
1. Introduction
As an important topic in analytical chemistry, ion-selective electrodes (ISEs) are indispensable members of the family of electrochemical sensors with the capability of electrochemically measuring primary ion activity in various sample matrices.1–4 Potentiometric measurement with ISEs is a non-destructive, passive analysis method that converts primary ion activity into electrical potential without the need for additional stimulation. Owing to their intrinsic characteristics of simplicity, low cost, small size, timeliness, and reliability, ISEs have been widely adopted as an option for rapid ion detection in the fields of biomedical, agricultural, environmental, and industrial analyses.5–8
The history of ISEs can be traced back to the beginning of the 20th century, when Cremer noticed that the electrical potential of a thin glass membrane varied with the pH of the solution.9 By the 1960s, the ISEs entered a critical period of development that laid the foundation for modern ISEs. Ever since Pungor pioneered halide-selective electrodes with heterogeneous membranes in 1961,10,11 several breakthrough inventions have ushered a new era in potentiometric analysis, including crystalline membrane ISEs,12 neutral ionophore-based liquid membrane ISEs,13,14 and charged ionophore-based liquid membrane ISEs.15 Besides, plasticized poly(vinyl chloride) (PVC) was gradually used as the standard matrix of the liquid membrane in this period, which still remains highly relevant today.16–18 Since then, these ISEs based on solid membranes (e.g., glassy membranes and crystalline membranes) or liquid membranes (e.g., polymeric membranes) have attracted extensive research attention and experienced a golden period of development up to now.19–23 Of these, polymeric membrane-based ISEs have gradually come to the fore due to their widest range of accessible ion selectivities. The ISEs based on ionophore-doped polymeric membranes (i.e., ion-selective membranes, ISMs) exhibit outstanding processibility and offer the possibility of detecting nearly one hundred different analytes, from inorganic to organic ions, and even some redox-inert species,24–26 which makes them the most promising and commonly used potentiometric ion sensors.
Conventional polymeric membrane-based ISEs are of liquid-contact type (liquid-contact ISEs) with inner filling solutions,27–33 following the design of early glass membrane electrodes. Although liquid-contact ISEs are relatively mature in routine detection and already commercially available, the presence of solution components limits their further development, as they require additional maintenance and thus are unfavorable for storage and miniaturization.1,34 Eventually, the total elimination of inner filling solutions results in solid-contact ISEs (SC-ISEs). The first SC-ISEs were proposed by Cattrall in 1971, known as coated wire electrodes (CWEs).35 However, it is difficult to achieve stable potential responses using these CWEs with simplified structures due to the ‘blocked’ interfaces between the ionically conducting ISM and electronically conducting substrate,36,37 and they suffer from the formation of oxygen half-cells38,39 and thin water layers.40–42 When the need for well-defined ion-to-electron transduction between two phases with different conductance characteristics was realized, the development of SC-ISEs began to make significant progress.43,44 Here, we present a brief historical timeline of SC-ISEs (Fig. 1).
 Fig. 1  A brief history of the key events in the development of SC-ISEs. 
Recently, as a new generation of potentiometric ion sensors, SC-ISEs have shown more broader application scope due to their unique advantages. A variety of solid-contact functional materials have been introduced into SC-ISEs as ion-to-electron transducers, especially numerous conductive polymers (CPs)45,46 and nanomaterials,47,48 which has further stimulated the potential of these sensors to achieve robustness and reliability, without the need for calibration and maintenance. Compared to conventional liquid-contact counterparts, polymeric membrane-based ISEs with the solid-contact configuration are easier to design and assemble to achieve miniaturization and flexibility and are compatible with modern printing and microfabrication technologies to produce sensor arrays at low cost and high throughput.34,49–51 Due to their unified production process and quality specifications, miniaturized SC-ISEs are capable of integrating with lab-on-chip devices and other advanced devices.51 In particular, the combination of SC-ISEs with wearable technologies has become a new important trend in this field, with great potential for commercialization.52,53 Besides, different strategies have also been adopted for further improvement of SC-ISEs in terms of sensitivity, selectivity, limit of detection (LOD), biocompatibility, biofouling, etc.54–58 Several excellent review articles on SC-ISEs have been published over the last few years with an emphasis on membrane materials, ionophores, and their applications in different fields.59–66 However, to the best of our knowledge, no comprehensive review article has summarized the far-reaching impact created by various solid-contact functional materials for SC-ISEs.
The significantly improved stability and reproducibility resulting from the use of solid-contact functional materials and a wide variety of miniaturized and flexible structures create new paradigms in the field of potentiometric ion sensors (Fig. 2). Therefore, a timely overview focusing on SC-ISEs is necessary to highlight their advantages and offer insights into strategies for further improvement of their performance and fabrication process for practical applications. In Section 2, we discuss the transduction mechanisms and performance criteria of SC-ISEs, as well as the required conditions for use of functional materials as ion-to-electron transducers. Section 3 covers the latest advances in solid-contact functional materials for SC-ISEs. In this section, the performance of SC-ISEs based on various functional materials is further discussed in detail. In Section 4, recent advances in SC-ISEs toward miniaturization and flexibility are highlighted, including the development of materials and process engineering, as well as the integration and applications of SC-ISEs in different fields. At last, we share our points on the development prospects and challenges for SC-ISEs and hope these discussions will further inspire more research and innovations in this rapidly evolving field.
 Fig. 2  An introduction to SC-ISEs: functional materials, sensor design, and their applications in the fields of routine analysis, lab on a chip, wearable point-of-care analysis, submersible measurement, potentiometric scanning measurement, and biomedical analysis. 
2. Transduction mechanisms and performance criteria of SC-ISEs
2.1 General principle
Potentiometric measurements are conducted in a two-electrode galvanic cell that converts the input ionic signal to a measurable electronic potential signal under open-circuit conditions.3 The electrical potential difference between the reference electrode (RE) and the working electrode (WE), i.e., the electromotive force (EMF), is usually determined using a voltmeter with high input impedance and low input bias current. This process does not involve redox reaction and requires no external driving force, which means passiveness, low-power consumption, and no consumption or contamination of the sample.67
The potential response of ISEs can be intuitively described according to the phase-boundary potential model, which is based on total equilibrium assumptions.68–70 Ideally, the measured potential originates from charge separation (nanometer dimensions) at the solution|membrane interface, which depends on the primary ion activity. This process is related to the surface chemisorption and equilibrium partitioning of primary ions, without the need for extensive transmembrane material transport.71 In the sample solution, the energy required for charge separation is provided by the chemical driving force (Gibbs free energy). Due to the permselectivity and ion selectivity of the ISMs, the primary ion can freely partition into and out of the membrane phase. When chemical driving forces counterbalance the opposing electrical Coulombic driving forces, the free diffusion of charged ions reaches an electrochemical equilibrium. At this time, an electrical double layer is formed at the solution|membrane interface, resulting in the electrical potential difference. If primary ion activity changes, this equilibrium will be quickly re-established and the phase boundary potential (EPB) at the solution|membrane interface will also change accordingly, which follows the equation shown below:3,72,73 (1)
In this equation, T, F, and R represent the absolute temperature, the Faraday constant, and the universal gas constant, respectively. kI is the ion-partition constant related to the free energy of the primary ion I. zI is the valency of the primary ion I, and if the analyte is an anion, its sign is negative. aI(aq) and aI(org) are the activities of primary ions I in the respective aqueous and organic phases.
The total EMF value is equal to the sum of all boundary potentials within the galvanic cell. Provided that the potentiometric system as a whole is in thermodynamic equilibrium and the electrochemical potential contributions of the remaining phase and interface to the EMF value are constant, within the galvanic cell only EPB at the solution|membrane interface varies. If aI(org) does not depend on the sample and is thus relatively constant, EPB depends only on aI in the aqueous phase, and therefore eqn (1) can be simplified to the well-known Nernst equation for ISEs:7,74 (2)where E0 is the standard potential of EMF (i.e., the intercept of the calibration curve) that can be gained when aI = 1. The constant ion activity in the membrane phase is generally ensured by the lipophilic ionic sites. According to the Nernst equation, a 10-fold primary ion activity change is followed by an increase in the potential response of 59.2/zI mV at room temperature (25 °C). For example, the ideal Nernstian slope for K+-ISEs is 59.2 mV per decade, whereas an EMF change of 1 mV approximately indicates a 4% change in activity.75 In practice, the actual response sensitivity of ISEs always shows a certain deviation from the ideal value and mainly depends on the intrinsic properties of the sensing membrane affected by the optimization conditions, membrane materials, ionophores, etc. For further improving the sensitivity of potentiometric ion sensing, several strategies have been proposed, including coulometric signal transduction,76 a joined electrochemical cell with multi-Nernstian slope,56 electronic integrated multi-electrode systems,77 electronic amplifier circuits,78 and so on.
It is worth noting that ISEs respond only to the free ion activity rather than the complex ion activity, but in most cases it is often justifiable to use molar concentrations instead of the primary ion activity. Besides, when the presence of interfering ions is considered, the Nernst formula for ISEs needs to be further extended to the semiempirical Nikolskii–Eisenman equation, involving the potentiometric selectivity coefficient KpotI,J.79 For the determination of selectivity coefficients, the commonly used methods are the separate solution method (SSM) and the fixed interference method (FIM), which have been described and recommended by the International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry (IUPAC).80–84 The high selectivity of a potentiometric probe is a key factor in ensuring its ability to distinguish between target ions and other interfering ions, which is especially desirable for the detection of ions in complex sample matrixes.25,55 For this pursuit, the synthesis and evaluation of new ionophores is of great importance, and more excellent work is needed in this field to stimulate greater vitality.85,86
The phase-boundary potential model provides a simple semiquantitative description of both liquid-contact and solid-contact ISEs and satisfactorily explains most of the experimental results in an intuitive and simple way, and thus has been widely applicable.74,87 But it is undeniable that this model is too idealistic and ignores many practical factors, such as the diffusion potential within the membrane and transmembrane ion fluxes.20,88,89 When it comes to better clarifying the complex, time-dependent potential behaviour of ISEs, advanced nonequilibrium models are certainly necessary.90–93 However, the existence of fewer restrictive model assumptions is at the price of an inevitable complexity. In a previous work, Bobacka et al. presented a detailed description and comment of the existing theoretical models for ISEs.3
Continuous research on theoretical models can not only help people better understand how ISEs work, but also stimulate innovations in this field. For example, breakthroughs in lowering the LOD of polymeric membrane-based ISEs originate from the detailed kinetic studies of transmembrane ion fluxes,94–99 which has significantly fuelled this field.100–102 These brilliant breakthroughs also further stimulate innovations for improving the LOD of solid-state membrane electrodes.22,23,103 In addition, the realization of the importance of ion fluxes has triggered dynamic electrochemistry for polymeric membrane-based ISEs.104,105 With the continuous in-depth study of the working mechanism of ISEs, alternative readout approaches have emerged, e.g., constant-current chronopotentiometry,106–108 ion transfer voltammetry,109–111 thin layer coulometry,112–114 constant potential coulometry,115–117 optical readout118–123 and wireless high-frequency readout.124 Dynamic electrochemical control techniques for ISEs have aroused great interest and have become a powerful complementary to classical zero-current potentiometry.62,125
2.2 Ion-to-electron transduction
The typical structure of SC-ISEs is a simple sandwich structure, including the conductive substrate at the bottom, the intermediate solid-contact layer, and the sensing membrane at the top, and so sometimes SC-ISEs can also be schematically represented as GC/GR/ISM (GC and GR represent glass carbon and graphene, respectively). In SC-ISEs, the ISM is the recognition element available for selective surface chemisorption of primary ions to quantify their activity and the solid contact (SC), i.e., the ion-to-electron transducer, is an electrochemical signal transducer that converts the input ionic signal to a measurable electrical potential signal. Due to the different inherent conductive properties of membranes and substrates, a well-defined pathway for ion-to-electron transduction is of critical importance to the stability and reproducibility of SC-ISEs. In conventional liquid-contact ISEs, the common combination of an internal Ag/AgCl reference electrode and the inner filling solution containing constant activities of primary ions and Cl− leads to a reversible redox reaction (AgCl(s) + e− ⇄ Ag(s) + Cl−(aq)) to achieve the equilibrium of charge transition from ionic to electronic conductivity. However, in the initial form of SC-ISEs, i.e., CWEs, this transduction process is “blocked” due to the poorly defined interfacial potential between the ISM and the underlying metal conductor, in which ions and electrons are strictly confined to their respective phases. In contrast, the well-defined ion-to-electron transduction processes of SC-ISEs are related to the presence of redox/double-layer capacitance at the ISM|SC interface, which is determined by the inherent characteristics of solid-contact functional materials acting as ion-to-electron transducers.126 These materials can be described as asymmetric capacitors based on redox/double-layer capacitance, allowing charging/discharging over a small but finite measuring current during potentiometric measurements.127,128

2.2.1 Redox capacitance. 
Electroactive materials are a large class of solid-contact functional materials used as ion-to-electron transducers in SC-ISEs, represented by CPs.104 The ion-to-electron transduction process of these functional materials is analogous to conventional liquid-contact ISEs containing internal reference systems, involving the reversible redox reaction Ox + e− ⇄ Red between the oxidizing species (Ox) and reducing species (Red). Fig. 3a shows the ion-to-electron transduction process of redox capacitance-based SC-ISEs. The primary ion I+ can reversibly bind to an ionophore (L) at the ISM|solution interface: I+(aq) + L(m) ⇄ I+L(m), where (aq) is the aqueous phase and (m) represents the membrane phase. The interfacial potential (φa1) is determined by the charge separation at this interface, which is related to primary ion activity. Meanwhile, I+ and lipophilic anionic sites R− can also reversibly transfer across the SC|ISM interface according to their respective ion mobilities, driving the local faradaic process (redox reaction) in the solid-contact layer. Eventually, electrons flow to the conductive substrate, enabling the electrical potential difference to be measured using the equipment. During this process, the interfacial potential (φa2) is controlled by the ion distribution between the transducer phase and membrane phase, whereas the interfacial potential (φa3) determined by the ratio of the Red/Ox of electroactive materials is related to the redox reaction. In CP-based SC-SEs, the redox reaction involves the reversible oxidation/reduction (doping/undoping) of the conjugated polymer backbone with simultaneous insertion/expulsion of anions and cations, which can be briefly expressed as follows:45,129 CP + A− ⇄ CP+A− + e−(3) CPA−I+ ⇄ CP+A− + I+ + e−(4)where CP is the neutral conducting polymer unit, CP+ is the oxidized conductive polymer unit and A− is the anion (e.g., the lipophilic ionic site in the ISM or the doping ion in CPs). The high redox capacitance of CPs relies on the redox buffering capacity that comes from their intrinsic properties of ionic and electronic conductivities, which can be influenced by the doping agents. Furthermore, through the study of the transduction mechanism of poly(3-octylthiophene) (POT), it can be inferred that the ion-to-electron transduction on the CP layer is a surface-confined charge-transfer process that only occurs on its surface, which depends on its surface reactivity and electrical capacitance, not thickness.130 In addition to CPs, other electroactive materials as solid-contact layers can also contribute to redox capacitance, such as ion exchange resins,131–134 redox-active self-assembled monolayers (SAMs),135–138 metallic or organic redox couples,139–143 redox couple-doped gold nanostructures,144,145 and so on.
 Fig. 3  Schematic representation of (a) an SC-ISE with a redox capacitance layer and (b) an SC-ISE with a double-layer capacitance layer. The cation-selective membrane contains an electrically neutral ionophore (L) and a lipophilic anionic site (R−). I+ is the primary ion. X− is the anion in solution. 


2.2.2 Double-layer capacitance. 
The same effect of stabilizing the interfacial potential can also be improved by applying SCs based on functional materials that have a large contact area with the ISM. In this type of ion-to-electron transduction, the conversion of charge carriers is not based on the redox activity of the functional material, but on the presence of large double-layer capacitance formed at the SC|ISM interface. As shown in Fig. 3b, charge carriers are asymmetrically distributed on both sides of the SC|ISM interface, in which the electrons/holes in the SC side and the anions/cations in the ISM side attract each other to form an electrical double layer. In this situation, the interfacial potential (φb2) is defined as the quantity of the charge in the electrical double layer. In early CWEs, although the asymmetric electrical double layer can also form at the interface between the metal wire and the ISM, the actual capacitance is quite small (only a few µF) due to the absence of effective ion-to-electron transduction. Therefore, the CWE behaves as a polarizable electrode without the ability to buffer any random tiny charge noise.
According to the relationship between the potential drift (dφ) and the accidental charge (dQ), i.e., dφ = C−1dQ, the increase of interfacial capacitance C allows enhanced potential stability.134 The main approach to increase the double-layer capacitance is to maximize the interfacial contact area between the solid-contact layer and the sensing membrane. Therefore, numerous micro/nanomaterials with high surface areas have been tried as ion-to-electron transducers, which will be discussed in detail in the next section. Besides, accompanied by the application of these micro/nanomaterials, the signal transduction mechanism based on double-layer capacitance has been further studied by electrochemical and spectroscopic techniques, revealing the effects of the surface chemical properties of functional materials, the interfacial contact area, and the chemisorption of charged carriers at the SC|ISM interface on the formation of double-layer capacitance.127,146,147 Ion-to-electron transduction based on double-layer capacitance is a purely capacitive transduction mechanism, in which functional materials do not exhibit redox behaviour. Using the cyclic voltammetry (CV) technique, the capacitance properties of different functional materials can be well distinguished.146

2.3 Stability and reproducibility of potential response
Whether in routine analysis or in vivo measurements, the stability and reproducibility of potential response is the key to ensure reliable measurement accuracy. In SC-ISEs, stable potential response means as low potential drift as possible, whereas poor potential stability generally indicates the inadequacy of the solid-contact layer. On the other hand, the potential reproducibility of an individual electrode determines its calibration frequency, while the E0 reproducibility between electrodes reflects whether they are directly interchangeable. An adequate solid-contact layer is one of the prerequisites for stable and reproducible potential response, whose role is to provide reversible ion-to-electron conversion and stabilize the phase boundary potential at the SC|ISM interface to maximize the potentiometric performance of SC-ISEs. The ultimate goal of developing a solid-contact layer is to fabricate highly stable, maintenance-free, calibration-free, and interchangeable potentiometric ion sensors.

2.3.1 Potential stability. 
When evaluating a functional material as the solid-contact layer, the most basic criterion is whether it contributes to the stable potential response of SC-ISEs, which requires a well-defined ion-to-electron transduction process. This process represents an asymmetric situation that generates a redox/double-layer capacitance, in which the input current of the measuring amplifier will inevitably result in its charging/discharging. In order to ensure the stable potential response of SC-ISEs, an ideal solid-contact layer should have an unpolarizable interface with sufficient high exchange current density. Although the polarization of electrodes is undesirable in practice, it can sometimes provide valuable information about the properties of the solid-contact layer and the stability of sensors. When considering the presence of an applied current, the low-frequency behaviour of SC-ISEs resembles an RC equivalent circuit in series, and the measured potential (E) can be expressed as follows:148 E = iRt + it/CL(5)where i is the applied current and t is the time of measurement. Rt is the total resistance of the electrode, which mainly depends on the bulk resistance of the ISM. CL represents the low-frequency capacitance of the electrode, which is essentially the redox/double-layer capacitance of the solid-contact layer. According to eqn (5), when the applied current is constant, the slope (ΔE/Δt) of the E–t curve, i.e., potential drift, can be expressed as: Potential drift = ΔE/Δt = i/CL(6)
This equation intuitively shows how CL and the measuring current affect the potential stability of SC-ISEs. Obviously, it can be found that when the measuring current is constant, a sufficiently high redox/double-layer capacitance of the solid-contact layer is a guarantee for protecting the electrode from polarization and achieving a stable potential response. When performing the direct potentiometric measurement under open-circuit conditions, the actual input current of high input impedance millivoltmeters is very small (only pA or less), and the solid-contact layer with large capacitance enables stable potential equilibrium.149 For example, if the expected potential drift of an SC-ISE is less than 1 mV h−1, which is expected to be used for long-term monitoring without frequent calibration, and the measuring current is 1 pA, the minimum theoretical CL required is 36 µF according to eqn (6). However, when a higher order of current is applied, a significant potential drift can be observed due to a certain degree of electrode polarization. Based on this, the influence of the applied current on the potential of SC-ISEs can be further studied by current-reversal chronopotentiometry (CRC) (Fig. 4),128 which is a convenient and quick method to assess electrode stability without performing the long-term test. When SC-ISEs are polarized with a steady-state current (usually 1 nA) for a certain period of time (usually 60 s for both positive and negative current), the Rt and CL of the electrode can be calculated based on the potential drift and the potential jump (ΔE = iRt), respectively. The results obtained from this method show a good correlation with those obtained by electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements.150 The CL of SC-ISEs obtained using different functional materials as solid-contact layers can be referred to the statistics in Table 1.
 Fig. 4  Typical chronopotentiograms for the CWE (red line) and the SC-ISE (blue line). When the applied current changes from +1 nA to −1 nA, a significant potential jump can be observed during the galvanostatic polarization process.

273





The magnitude of CL has become a very important indicator of the potential stability of SC-ISEs. Nevertheless, it cannot be ignored that the potential drift is caused by a variety of factors. From the perspective of the solid-contact layer, except for undesirable polarization, the possible formation of a water layer at the SC|ISM interface and the side reactions with light, redox couples, O2, and CO2 are also adverse factors, which would compromise the stability of the phase boundary potential and even cause the invalidation of the ion-to-electron transducer. On the other hand, as another important component of SC-ISEs, the ISM determines the potentiometric characteristics and lifetime of the sensor.24 Beneficial improvements in ISMs, such as the use of solid ionophores, will lead to a significantly longer lifetime and better sensitivity.28,29,32 However, the changes in the membrane composition can also affect potential stability and analytical performance, which may be caused by the membrane itself (e.g., the leaching and decomposition of its constituents) or by the solid-contact layer.151 For example, it is reported that the interfering ion at the back of the membrane resulting from the solid-contact layer152,153 and the interference of the solid-contact layer with the concentration of primary ions in the membrane can change the potentiometric performance of SC-ISEs.154 In addition, solid-contact functional materials may dissolve and permeate into the ISM, e.g., POT can spontaneously dissolve into the membrane phase,155,156 which may also occur in gold nanoclusters (AuNCs).145 When it comes to potential drift, the requirements should be matched to specific applications, differentiating between short- or long-term stability requirements to select the most appropriate design. Certainly, ensuring optimized manufacturing conditions is a prerequisite necessary to obtain the final sensors with stable performance.157


2.3.2 
            E
0 reproducibility. 
Sensor calibration is the most common and necessary pretreatment protocol before the potentiometric measurement to ensure the reproducibility and validity of analytical results. Although the periodic calibration of SC-ISEs with low potential drift is sufficient to meet the analytical requirements in many practical applications, the pursuit of decreasing calibration procedures to achieve calibration-free measurements or at least infrequent single-point calibration has long been the expectation of researchers.158 Complex or frequent calibration is not user-friendly, which usually consumes time, increases costs, and requires the users to have some expertise. Particularly, recently growing interest in disposable and wearable sensors calls for affordable, calibration-free, and easy-to-use devices.52,159–161 SC-ISEs have unique advantages in calibration-free measurements, because they usually have high selectivity for primary ions and their potential response follows the Nernst rule, that is, the slope of the linear range of the calibration curve is very close to the ideal Nernstian slope.162 However, it is still a challenge for SC-ISEs to achieve a high reproducibility of the standard potential E0, which is another key requirement for calibration-free measurements. Unlike the Nernstian slope, the value of standard potential E0 is not a priori known and is difficult to predict theoretically. Generally, the E0 values of SC-ISEs are obtained by extrapolation of the linear range of the calibration curve to 1.0 M primary ion concentration, but even in one batch under exactly the same manufacturing conditions in the laboratory, their values often vary from electrode to electrode. E0 irreproducibility means that each electrode needs to be individually calibrated before use and cannot be directly interchanged, which is also considered as the probable biggest challenge hampering SC-ISEs form being commercialized as disposable or wearable sensors.
In recent years, since the potential stability of SC-ISEs has been significantly improved, increasing attention has been to be paid to the improvement of the E0 reproducibility between electrodes. However, as the group of Bühlmann pointed out, the standard potential E0 of an SC-ISE is determined by the intrinsic characteristics of the entire galvanic cell architecture, including each phase and interface.126 Ensuring small E0 variation requires not only a well-defined SC|ISM interface, but also good control of the rest. Although, for an individual electrode with low potential drift, the constant intercept of the calibration curve over a period of time is relatively easy to achieve, when it comes to the E0 reproducibility between electrodes, the situation will be more complex, in which any differences between each other will be amplified and result in large E0 deviation. Therefore, it makes more sense to test the E0 reproducibility between electrodes than to test the calibration slope reproducibility of electrodes. Ever since this concept emerged, although still in its infancy, the E0 reproducibility of SC-ISEs has been significantly improved through different strategies, e.g., electrochemical control of the solid-contact layer or by exploring new functional materials. Along with increasing potential stability, these efforts to manufacture SC-ISEs with high E0 reproducibility have continuously pushed potentiometric ion sensors towards achieving maintenance-free and calibration-free measurements. What is certain is that an adequate ion-to-electron transducer that can ensure stable phase boundary potential is still a key essential factor.
When CPs are used as solid-contact layers, they can exist in a rather broad range of redox states with different doping levels.274 Nevertheless, this unique property also contributes to the undesirable time-varying electrical potential, accompanied by the unpredictable E0 deviation between electrodes (can reach tens of millivolts). In the electropolymerization process of CPs by galvanostatic,275,276 potentiostatic,277,278 or CV methods,279,280 the redox state of the film can be determined by external control factors. Although the potential of CPs will spontaneously drift toward open-circuit equilibrium after preparation,157 these methods are found to be still useful as pretreatments to enhance potential reproducibility.281 Based on the idea of controlling the position of the calibration slope by adjusting the redox states of CPs, the group of Bobacka proposed an electrochemical control method to tune the standard potential E0 of CP-based SC-ISEs via an external potential or a nanoamp-range current.282 Poly(3,4-ethylene dioxythiophene) doped with poly(sodium 4-styrenesulfonate), i.e., PEDOT/PSS, was selected as a model transducer due to its stability in its oxidized (p-doped) state. Since the changes in the redox state and the ionic content in the CP layer require the transfer of ions with the membrane, the composition of the ISM will affect the tunability of the standard potential E0. In addition, they also offered a simple, instrument-free, but relatively time-consuming means to control the standard potential of PEDOT/PSS-based SC-ISEs by a so-called short-circuiting method, which used a metallic wire to connect the SC-ISE and the RE immersed in the primary ion solution of certain concentration.283 During this process, due to the continuous oxidation/reduction of the PEDOT/PSS layer, the potential of the SC-ISE will shift near to that of the RE. However, since the SC-ISEs will spontaneously re-drift toward an equilibrium state, the hold time for the adjusted standard potential is limited.
The potentiostatic prepolarization of the CP layer can bring it in a specific redox state, thereby improving potential reproducibility.284 This process is performed prior to the deposition of the membrane cocktail and thus does not involve ion transfer between the solid-contact layer and the sensing membrane. The group of Lindfors further demonstrated the enhanced effect of prepolarization on the E0 reproducibility of SC-ISEs in the case of polyazulene,285 polypyrrole (PPY),286 and PEDOT as the solid-contact layers.287 Among them, the best E0 reproducibility is obtained with PPY-based SC-ISEs, for which the standard deviation (SD) of E0 is reported to be ±0.7 mV (n = 4) which can maintain a relatively long-term stability of ±3.1 mV after storage in 10−4 M KCl solution for 46 days.286 According to the existing reports about CP-based SC-ISEs, the high E0 reproducibility obtained in this work is the best one. However, the authors also pointed out that this high E0 reproducibility between electrodes was obtained under optimal conditions in the same batch; however, achieving such outstanding reproducibility between batches still remains a challenge. They also prepared a highly hydrophobic solid-contact layer (contact angle 133°) based on tetrakis(pentafluorophenyl)borate (TFAB−) anion perfluorinated alkanoate side chain functionalized PEDOT (PEDOTF–TFAB).287 Through the prepolarization of the solid-contact layer, the E0 reproducibility (SD) of the prepared K+-ISEs reached ±3.0 mV (n = 5). Besides, the authors believed that potential irreproducibility mainly comes from ISM deposition or its inhomogeneity, which is determined by comparing the potential reproducibility of the polymer-modified electrodes before and after the deposition of the membrane cocktail. Apart from these abovementioned electrochemical control methods, Joon et al. proposed a simple outer coating method by coating an outer layer of pure silicone rubber (SR) on the top of PVC membrane SC-ISEs to improve their analytical performance and reduce biofouling.288 Through this method, it is found that the E0 reproducibility (SD) of the treated SC-ISEs increases to ±3.5 mV (n = 4) compared to ±35.3 mV for the conventional PEDOT-based counterparts after conditioning in 10−3 M KCl solution for 8 days.
For functional materials with high surface areas, such as carbon micro/nanomaterials, stable phase boundary potential at the SC|ISM interface is generally based on a purely capacitive transduction mechanism. In the case of colloid-imprinted mesoporous carbon (CIMC), it has been shown that the presence of redox-active surface functional groups and impurities can affect the E0 reproducibility between electrodes.172 This seems to be extrapolated to other solid-contact functional materials, because despite the obvious advances in the potential stability of SC-ISEs achieved by the introduction of carbon micro/nanomaterials, there are no examples claiming that SC-ISEs with the use of a single carbon micro/nanomaterial have superior E0 reproducibility. In this regard, the incorporation of other materials into carbon micro/nanomaterials seems to be an attractive approach to better control the interfacial potential between SC|ISM to improve the E0 reproducibility. For instance, for GR-based SC-ISEs, from the statistical data in Table 1, it is observed that SC-ISEs using GR composites are more promising to achieve lower E0 reproducibility. For example, solid-contact Ag+-ISEs with the solid-contact layer of silver tetrakis[3,5-bis(trifluoromethyl)phenyl]borate-modified GR (GR–AgTFPB) exhibit a low batch-to-batch E0 deviation of 1.26 mV.221 When GR is combined with other functional materials, such as polyaniline (PANI),222 platinum nanoparticles (PtNPs),235,236 tetrathiafulvalene (TTF),253 and 7,7,8,8-tetracyanoquinodimethane (TCNQ),258,259 enhanced E0 reproducibility is also observed. Paczosa-Bator used a relatively complex composition containing porous GR, carbon black (CB), and a fluorinated acrylic copolymer as the solid-contact layer for K+-ISEs.226 In the best case, the E0 reproducibility (SD) can be as low as ±0.1 mV (n = 5). It is worth noting that in this study, CB and GR also achieve high E0 reproducibility when used as the solid-contact layer alone. However, it is unclear whether such high reproducibility is also achievable between batches. About the improvement of E0 reproducibility, the development of SC-ISEs based on carbon nanotubes (CNTs) is similar to that of GR-based SC-ISEs. When cobalt(II) porphyrin/cobalt(III) corrole is introduced into the solid-contact layer, a high E0 reproducibility (SD) of CNT-based SC-ISEs is obtained (±0.7 mV, n = 4).252 Interestingly, the group of Gyurcsányi has recently demonstrated that the initial interelectrode E0 deviation of CNT-based SC-ISEs can be further effectively reduced by means of external polarization or short-circuiting, which also proves that these two methods are effective for SC-ISEs based on the purely capacitive transduction mechanism.289
Among those materials used to form composites with carbon micro/nanomaterials, the most attractive approach to improve E0 reproducibility may be the use of a variety of low molecular weight redox couples, which have a well-controlled concentration ratio of oxidized/reduced species to stabilize the electrical potential of the redox buffer layer that followed the Nernst equation. As one of the components of composite materials, molecular redox couples are also used to dope the plasticized PVC membrane or directly as the solid-contact layer. When these materials are introduced into the SC-ISEs in either way, the E0 reproducibility of these sensors is generally claimed to be close to or less than 1 mV, just like the examples of the Co2+/Co3+ redox couple,140,172,251 cobalt(II) porphyrin/cobalt(III) corrole pair (not a redox couple),152,252 TCNQ,141,256,290 and TTF.143,253,255 More details about the applications of these redox couples or related materials will be discussed in a separate section on molecular redox couples.
For SC-ISEs with other electroactive materials as solid-contact layers, it has been reported that the solid-contact K+-ISEs using a redox buffer layer of monolayer-protected AuNCs have a high E0 reproducibility (SD) of ±0.8 mV.144 Solid-contact K+-ISEs using KBr/AgBr as the solid-contact layer exhibit an E0 reproducibility (SD) of ±1.7 mV.260 In the case of intercalation compounds (ICs) LiFePO4/FePO4 as solid-contact layers, Li+-ISEs can achieve an E0 reproducibility (SD) of ±2.0 mV (n = 3).142 Similarly, when the target ion intercalated Prussian blue analogues (PBAs) are used as solid-contact layers, SC-ISEs show a comparable E0 reproducibility to their counterparts with inner filling solutions.264 For these ion-intercalated materials, the stable phase boundary potential is derived from the constant activity of primary ions present in the ISM and transducer phase.
Although the SC|ISM interface is a key factor for the stability and reproducibility of potential response, it cannot be ignored that another interface between the solid-contact layer and the underlying conductive substrate is also important. The selection of conductive substrates can also affect the potential stability and reproducibility of SC-ISEs. In previous works, Bobacka et al. studied the effect of the substrate material on the potential stability and found that SC-ISEs constructed on GC electrodes, compared to Au or Pt electrodes, are not influenced by the partial pressure of oxygen in the solution.291 Mousavi et al. found that the long-term potential stability of SC-ISEs depends on the conductive substrate used to deposit the solid-contact layer, and CNT modified screen-printed electrodes show a better performance than GC electrode-based ones.199 In another study, the group of Lindner compared the equilibration time of SC-ISEs constructed on the GC, Au, or Pt electrodes and noted the differences resulting from the equilibration process at the interface between the substrate electrode and the solid-contact layer.292 It is also found that the selection of different substrate materials can affect the E0 reproducibility of SC-ISEs.140,286,287,293 The group of Bühlmann demonstrated that when redox buffer capacity was sufficient to stabilize the phase boundary potential, the E0 difference caused by different substrate materials can be greatly reduced.140 The group of Lindner also observed a similar effect of reducing differences resulting from the substrate materials by incorporating a redox couple into the solid-contact layer.294 Although, in principle, the phase boundary potential does not depend on the type of conductive substrate used, its intrinsic properties (e.g., electrical conductivity and surface chemistry) and uncontrolled surface redox-active impurities are also significant and therefore cannot be used equivalently. When designing and manufacturing SC-ISEs, it may be necessary to carefully consider the quality of the substrate electrode and ensure thorough cleaning of the electrode surface.

2.4 The invalidation of ion-to-electron transducers

Water layer. 
The key to the development of polymeric membrane-based ISEs from the liquid-contact to solid-contact configuration is the replacement of inner filling solutions with adequate solid-contact functional materials. However, when an undesirable water layer (also known as the aqueous layer) forms at the SC|ISM interface, the ISEs with the solid-contact configuration once again have a liquid component, which is just like the inner filling solution but without any reference elements.41 Far from providing a reversible phase boundary potential, the presence of a continuous water layer between the solid-contact layer and the sensing membrane irreversibly deteriorates the long-term potential stability of SC-ISEs by acting as a reservoir for transmembrane ions and neutral species with changeable compositions.42,295 In this case, the ion-to-electron transducer is considered to have partly failed. As the water layer continues to spread across the interface, the adhesion between the sensing membrane and solid-contact layer will be further destroyed, eventually leading to mechanical failure by delamination.
The phenomenon of water uptake in polymeric membranes has been recognized long ago.296–300 In 2000, Fibbioli et al. further demonstrated the possible presence of a water layer between the sensing membrane and the underlying metal substrate.41 Through theoretical model derivation, Morf et al. demonstrated that the water layer with a small volume would lead to a drastic potential drift of SC-ISEs.301 It is generally believed that the uptake and diffusion of water in the polymeric membrane is the main reason for the formation of a water layer, which has also been well studied by different characterization techniques, such as EIS and Fourier transform infrared attenuated total reflection (FTIR-ATR) spectroscopy.302–307 According to the results of neutron reflectometry, the thickness of a water layer is on the order of just 100 Å.42 The extremely small volume of a water layer means that it is much more sensitive to the changes in the composition and osmolality of the sample solution in comparison to the conventional inner filling solution or a hydrogel with a larger aqueous volume.
In order to eliminate the water layer, one approach is to use alternative membrane matrixes with smaller water diffusion coefficients, such as polyurethane (PU)136 and copolymer methyl methacrylate–decyl methacrylate (MMA–DMA).308 However, in the absence of a hydrophobic solid-contact layer, an undesirable water layer may still be observed even though such polymeric matrixes are water-repellent.309,310 In this situation, transmembrane water tends to pool in the contact defect areas between the polymeric membrane and the underlying metal substrate. On the other hand, with the employment of potassium hexacyanoferrate(II)/(III) (HCF) doped PPY as the solid-contact layer, conventional highly plasticized PVC membrane-based SC-ISEs have successfully avoided the formation of a water layer.311,312 Given all that, it seems that a hydrophobic solid-contact layer in good contact with the sensing membrane is essential to inhibit the formation of a water layer. In this regard, different functional materials with enhanced hydrophobicity have been used as solid-contact layers and have been found to exhibit an excellent effect of suppressing the formation of a water layer.249,287,313 However, it has also been found that certain surface properties of the solid-contact layer increase the likelihood of water layer formation to some extent, such as entrapped salts of CPs,312 residual dispersing agents,314 a high number of edge defects,173 and hydrophilic functional groups147 on the surface of the carbon nanomaterials.
In addition, from the perspective of the fabrication protocol of SC-ISEs, water may already exist during the manufacturing process, such as during the drop-casting of aqueous/organic solution or electrodeposition in aqueous solution.295 Another concern is that, compared to the relatively slow process of water transmembrane transport, in the case of the impaired sealing at the edge of the membrane, water will enter the back side of the membrane from the contact defects and form a continuous water layer more quickly. Therefore, it is necessary to ensure that the solid-contact layer is thoroughly dried before covering the membrane cocktail and that there is sufficient adhesion strength between the membrane, the solid-contact layer, and the underlying substrate to eliminate any possible unintended water source.
To assess the possible presence of a water layer in polymeric membrane-based SC-ISEs, the commonly used method is the so-called water layer test (Fig. 5a).41,301 Although this method has a simple test protocol, it is relatively time-consuming, usually requiring a dozen hours, and takes a much longer equilibrium time when testing polymeric membranes with low water diffusion coefficients. Moreover, the selection of appropriate interfering ions is important for the water layer test, because highly discriminated ions cannot produce enough ion exchange-induced fluxes. In addition to the method based on transmembrane ion fluxes, the presence of a water layer can also be confirmed by recording the potential drifts of SC-ISEs in the sample solutions with different CO2 levels (Fig. 5b).138,313 The easy transmembrane diffusion of CO2 into the water layer will change its local pH, thereby leading to a chemical hysteresis. This method requires a relatively short test time (even minutes),313 but is generally used for the SC-ISEs with the H+-ISM. Lindner et al. have previously provided a comprehensive description and comment on the approaches to assess the possible presence of a water layer.295 Recently, the same group has re-evaluated the widely used water layer test through both experiments and simulations and proposed some new recommendations, emphasizing the importance of a sufficient electrode preconditioning time and experimental equilibration time.315 Anyway, the removal of a water layer is of critical importance for achieving true SC-ISEs without any aqueous components. The ability to inhibit the formation of a water layer has become an indispensable part of the comprehensive performance evaluation of a new solid-contact functional material.
 Fig. 5  (a) Theoretical potential response curve of the SC-ISEs in the presence of a thin water layer (red line) or in the absence of a thin layer water (blue line) during the water layer test. (b) Schematic illustration of the test for the presence of a water layer based on the transmembrane diffusion of CO2. When a water layer forms at the backside of the H+-ISM, the corresponding changes in the pH value caused by the increase of CO2 concentration in the sample solution will result in a significant negative potential drift. 


External interference. 
In addition to the interference from the formation of a water layer, some external interferences, such as light, CO2, O2, and redox couples, can also interfere with the ion-to-electron transduction process, which requires that the solid-contact functional materials have sufficient capacitance and chemical stability to contend. The light sensitivity of CP-based SC-ISEs is mainly related to the intrinsic photovoltaic properties of CPs, which are also actually photosensitive semiconductors. In general, the influence of light on the potential stability of CP-based SC-ISEs is relatively limited in the absence of strong light exposure. In a previous work, Lindfors has tested and compared the photosensitivity of commonly used CPs in an extreme situation when the solid-contact layer is in direct contact with water and found that POT shows the highest light sensitivity, while PPY shows the best potential stability upon illumination with light of high intensity.284 When functional materials without photovoltaic effects are used as solid-contact layers, the potential response of SC-ISEs is generally unaffected by light.203,228 The gas sensitivity of solid-contact layers to O2 and CO2 is associated with harmful side reactions. The transmembrane diffusion of O2 can affect the phase boundary potential by forming an irreversible oxygen half-cell,168 which can act as an unwanted redox interference to redox-active species in the membrane phase and transducer phase. In CP-based SC-ISEs, the presence of the O2/H2O redox couple316 and O2/CP charge-transfer complexes317 is a possible adverse factor affecting the potential stability. In the presence of dissolved O2 and CO2, PEDOT is proved to be more stable than PPY.318 When a water layer is present in the back of the ISM, it is sensitive to the changes in the CO2 partial pressure. In particular, the interactions of CO2 with surface functional groups of hydrophobic materials may also lead to the gas sensitivity of the SC-ISEs, even in the absence of a water layer. For example, compared to three-dimensionally ordered macroporous carbon (3DOMC),168 CIMC with higher purity and fewer surface functional groups shows better stability in the gas interference test.172 Interestingly, porous carbon sub-micrometer spheres (PC-SMSs) with higher hydrophobicity exhibit a similar potential drift to 3DOMC when exposed to dissolved CO2, which is possibly due to the active functional groups on their surface.165 When considering the redox couples (e.g., Fe2+/Fe3+) in the sample solution, the inert PVC membrane can generally protect the inner redox-sensitive layers from any adverse effects.319 Therefore, from the point of view of avoiding redox interference, the sensing membrane should contain as little redox substance as possible.320 For example, redox sensitivity is observed when POT is used as an additive in the membrane,321 but not when POT is present as a separate layer.322

2.5 Prerequisites for solid-contact functional materials
The need for solid-contact layers in polymeric membrane-based ISEs mainly stems from two aspects, one is to facilitate manufacturing and miniaturization and the other is to optimize the ion-to-electron transduction process. Although the conventional liquid-contact ISEs can be precisely machined to very small sizes, the process requirements are very demanding. In contrast, for SC-ISEs, the desire to achieve miniaturization and flexibility does not seem to be difficult. The elimination of the inner filling solution and inner reference electrode allows SC-ISEs to have more flexible structural designs and simpler production requirements and show compatibility with modern planar processing technologies, which is also one of the reasons why SC-ISEs are now popular.
With the ongoing in-depth discussion and research on these SC-ISEs, the regularity of electrode potential formation and the quality control criteria for stable SC-ISEs have been better understood.3,5,126,295 Several prerequisites should be fully considered before trying new functional materials as solid-contact layers:37 (1) as the ion-to-electron transducer, the most basic function of the solid-contact layer is to provide a sufficiently fast and reversible transition from ion to electron conduction without any parallel side reactions. (2) An ideal solid-contact layer should possess a non-polarizable interface with large redox/double-layer capacitance to protect against the thin current flow applied during potentiometry and have the ability to re-establish the equilibrium in the presence of some external disturbances. (3) A well-defined interface potential between the solid-contact layer and the sensing membrane is the key to ensure potential stability and the E0 reproducibility between electrodes. (4) The chemical stability of solid-contact functional materials is very essential, which is related to their lifetime and the possible side reactions of SC-ISEs with light, gas, and redox species. Sufficient hydrophobicity and low water absorption are conducive to inhibiting the formation of a water layer and preventing the deformation of the solid-contact layer. (5) The implementation of functional materials should be independent of the composition of the sensing membrane and should not lead to uncontrolled impurities on the back side of the ISM that affect the EMF response. (6) From the design and manufacturing perspectives, the functional materials for the solid-contact layer should be readily available, economical, nontoxic, and capable of simplifying sensor fabrication.
3. Solid-contact functional materials used as ion-to-electron transducers
3.1 Conductive polymers
Since the early 1990s, the introduction of a variety of CPs as ion-to-electron transducers for SC-ISEs has made remarkable progress in this field, especially represented by PPY,319 POT,291 PANI,321 and PEDOT.128 These materials can be generally divided into two main types according to their oxidation (p-doping) levels. Among them, PPY, PANI, and PEDOT typically possess relative stability in their highly p-doped states, whereas POT is stable in its undoped form with a low redox capacitance and electronic conductivity. This also implies two different transduction paths: the former is based on high redox capacitance and electronic conductivity, while the latter is mainly ascribed to the formation of an electrical double layer between the POT layer and the ISM.126 These CPs have their own unique properties and are suitable for certain application conditions; however, due to more or less restrictive factors, none of them have been widely accepted as universal solid-contact functional materials yet.
As for highly p-doped CPs, they are generally electroactive over a broad range of potentials with the possible occurrence of parallel side reactions during the process of ion-to-electron transduction, resulting in long-term potential drift. For instance, PANI is known to be a highly pH-sensitive material due to its pH-dependent transition from the electrically conducting emeraldine salt form to the nonconducting emeraldine base form, which is considered to be an adverse factor for the long-term stability of PANI-based SC-ISEs.323,324 Therefore, PANI is relatively less attractive as the solid-contact layer when compared with other CPs. In other cases, PPY is sensitive to certain common reactants (e.g., H+, OH−, and O2),325 and it is demonstrated that the PEDOT-based SC-ISEs are more stable than their PPY-based counterparts when exposed to dissolved O2 and CO2 in the sample solution.318 In addition, these CPs are relatively hydrophilic due to a larger charge density on their polymeric backbone.286 For example, although no continuous water layer is formed between the PEDOT/PSS layer and the sensing membrane, the PEDOT/PSS layer exhibits a hydrogel-like behavior, becoming hydrated and swollen.326,327 Besides, the synthesis methods (i.e., electrical or chemical polymerization) can affect the water solubility of the deposited PEDOT/PSS film. Generally, the electrodeposited PEDOT/PSS film is considered to have a lower water solubility and better adhesion than the solution-cast one. However, the electrosynthesis approach may not be suitable for certain manufacturing technologies, such as large-scale ink printing technology.328 On the other hand, the water solubility of the chemically polymerized PEDOT/PSS film can be decreased by using ionic cross-linkers, which enable the PEDOT-based aqueous inks to possess certain advantages for mass production as the solid-contact layers for SC-ISEs.329,330 In contrast to highly p-doped CPs, POT is more lipophilic and less subject to side reactions. The common approach to prepare the POT solid-contact layer is the drop-cast method to avoid the high charge density on the POT film.308 However, the intrinsically low redox capacitance and electronic conductivity of its commonly used undoped form make the POT solid-contact layer more sensitive to external electrical noise.3 Besides, the application of POT as the ion-to-electron transducer for SC-ISEs is also limited by its light sensitivity303 and spontaneous partition into the membrane phase.156
In order to overcome these known disadvantages of CPs and further improve the performance characteristics of these CP-based SC-ISEs, different strategies have been adopted, including hydrophobic CP derivatives and lipophilic dopants,286,287,313 nanostructured CPs,331–334 nanocomposites containing CPs,200,216,250,273 external electrochemical control or prepolarization,282,286 dynamic electrochemistry protocols,115,125 and so on. Besides, a lot of works have been conducted to further study and optimize different potentiometric ion sensing systems with the use of CP-based SC-ISEs as model potentiometric probes.58,335,336 Some interesting applications of CP-based SC-ISEs have also emerged, such as ion-selective pumps for ionic transport and separation.337,338 Undoubtedly, the application and investigation of CPs as ion-to-electron transducers for SC-ISEs has revealed their current irreplaceable significance. Other application approaches of CPs in potentiometric ion sensors, e.g., as sensing membranes, have also attracted extensive attention.339–341 As an important and huge branch of this field, there have been many excellent review articles related to the development of CP-based SC-ISEs due to their self-evident importance.3,61,150,342–345 If readers want to get a more comprehensive understanding of these CP-based SC-ISEs, these works will be a treasure house.
3.2 Carbon micro/nanomaterials

3.2.1 Spherical carbon micro/nanomaterials. 
Fullerenes (C60) are zero-dimensional hollow spherical carbon molecules with polygonal faces346 and can act as efficient charge-transfer mediators and allow the reversible acceptance of several electrons.347–349 In 2008, Fouskaki et al. fabricated solid-contact K+-ISEs based on C60-modified GC electrodes (Fig. 6a).163 Although pristine C60 is not an electrically conducting material, the facilitation of interfacial charge transfer between the ISM and the underlying GC electrode depends on the redox ability of the C60 electroactive layer to reversibly accept and give electrons, as demonstrated by the EIS measurement. The C60-based SC-ISEs to some extent show a better potential stability than CWEs. With the deepening of research on C60, it is found that a C60 derivative with a two-dimensional (2D) hexagonal plate-like morphology and enhanced electrochemically active surface area can be obtained through introducing multiple pores in pristine C60 crystals, which is termed as bimodal pore C60 (BP-C60).350 In 2015, the group of Qin fabricated a solid-contact Pb2+-ISE by depositing the membrane cocktail onto the BP-C60 layer, which was electrodeposited onto the GC electrode (Fig. 6b).164 The results of the CV test show that the capacitive current of the macro-pore BP-C60 modified GC electrode is higher than that of the electrodes modified with either meso-pore BP-C60 or pristine C60. Besides, the excellent potentiometric performance of the SC-ISEs based on macro-pore BP-C60 also proves it to be an effective ion-to-electron transducer. A low LOD of 5.0 × 10−10 M for Pb2+ is obtained in this study.
 Fig. 6  (a) SEM image of the C60 crystal. Reproduced with permission.163 Copyright 2008, Royal Society of Chemistry. (b) SEM image of the BP-C60 crystal. Reproduced with permission.164 Copyright 2015, Elsevier. (c) SEM image of PC-SMSs. Reproduced with permission.165 Copyright 2015, Elsevier. (d) TEM images of SHCNs with different numbers of shells. Scale bar, 100 nm. Reproduced with permission.166 Copyright 2019, American Chemical Society. 
In addition to spherical C60, some other spherical carbon materials have also been implemented in SC-ISEs as internal transducer layers. For example, Niu et al. introduced PC-SMSs as ion-to-electron transducers for the fabrication of K+-ISEs (Fig. 6c).165 The PC-SMSs are uniformly porous spherical materials with diameters of about 650 nm, characterized by a large surface area, high electrical conductivity, and hydrophobicity (contact angle 137°). The high capacitance of the PC-SMS film on the GC electrode is demonstrated by EIS and CV tests. Moreover, the SC-ISEs constructed using PC-SMSs possess good resistance to O2 interference and the formation of a water layer. But these SC-ISEs exhibit a certain degree of gas sensitivity when exposed to a high concentration of dissolved CO2. The group of Mao proposed a miniaturized Ca2+-ISE based on shelled hollow carbon nanosphere (SHCN) modified carbon fiber electrodes (CFEs) (Fig. 6d).166 The performances of SC-ISEs with different shell amounts of SHCNs were evaluated, and the SHCNs with three shells were selected to prepare Ca2+-ISEs due to their best potentiometric performance. The final obtained Ca2+-ISE is proved to have good potential stability and interference immunity, and the formation of a water layer is inhibited due to the hydrophobic hollow structure of SHCNs. Jiang et al. fabricated solid-contact K+-ISEs using ordered mesoporous carbon spheres (OMCSs) as ion-to-electron transducers.167 The synthesized OMCSs were firstly dispersed in a mixture of ethanol and Nafion and then dropped onto the surface of the GC electrode to prepare the solid-contact layer. When the carbonization temperature is 750 °C, the optimal performance of OMCS-based SC-ISEs is obtained, including higher potential stability and reproducibility. Besides, flexible OMCS-based SC-ISEs and REs on polyethylene terephthalate (PET) substrates were also fabricated for combination with handheld ion sensing systems.


3.2.2 Porous carbon micro/nanomaterials. 
3DOMC consists of a glassy carbon skeleton surrounding a periodic array of uniform spherical holes connected to each other in three dimensions. The typical pore diameter of 3DOMC is about hundreds of nanometers, while the thickness of the skeleton wall is tens of nanometers. In 2007, the group of Bühlmann utilized 3DOMC fabricated by the colloidal crystal templating method as the solid-contact layer to prepare solid-contact K+-ISEs.168 As an early attempt to use carbon micro/nanomaterials as solid-contact layers, this design method shows great potential. During the long-term potential stability test, the 3DOMC-based SC-ISEs exhibit a low potential drift of 11.7 µV h−1 and are insensitive to the interference from O2, light, and redox couples. However, obvious CO2 sensitivity is observed even in the absence of a water layer. Later, the same group further optimized the design of 3DOMC-based SC-ISEs, resulting in low LODs of 4.0 × 10−11 M and 1.6 × 10−7 M for Ag+ and K+, respectively.169 The ion-to-electron transduction mechanism of the 3DOMC layer has also been investigated, indicating that the large double-layer capacitance comes from the large interfacial contact area between the plasticized PVC membranes and the pores of the 3DOMC (Fig. 7a and b).147 Besides, the experimental results on the influence of the surface chemistry of 3DOMC on the performance of SC-ISEs show that oxidation enables the 3DOMC to possess a higher surface functional group concentration, and therefore the 3DOMC-based SC-ISEs are more likely to form an aqueous layer, causing deterioration in stability and possible delamination of the ISM.147 In addition, the same group also prepared 3DOMC-based SC-ISEs with fluorous sensing membranes for the detection of perfluorocarbon (PFC) surfactants, which showed LODs at the nanomolar level.171 Their excellent performance in the analysis of lake water samples indicates that these SC-ISEs have application potential for simple on site pre-screening of large numbers of samples prior to other conventional analysis methods.
 Fig. 7  (a) SEM image of a 3DOMC electrode infiltrated by the PVC-based ISM in a sample holder, and (b) the partial enlarged view of * location (right). Reproduced with permission.147 Copyright 2010, American Chemical Society. (c) Schematic diagram of CIMC-based SC-ISEs. Reproduced with permission.172 Copyright 2014, American Chemical Society. (d) Photograph of a paper-based ion-sensing platform containing a CIMC-based SC-ISE and a CIMC-based SC-RE. (e) Cross-sectional view of the CIMC-based SC-ISE. Reproduced with permission.257 Copyright 2018, American Chemical Society. (f) SEM image of PC and (g) cross-sectional views of the open porous structure of PC without or with filling with the sensing membrane. Reproduced with permission.173 Copyright 2014, Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. 
These unique properties of 3DOMC have attracted the researchers' interest for the application of porous carbon micro/nanomaterials in SC-ISEs. In 2014, the group of Bühlmann proposed another porous carbon micro/nanomaterial, i.e., CIMC, as the solid-contact layer of SC-ISEs (Fig. 7c).172 With a similar structure to 3DOMC, however, CIMC has a higher purity, lower redox-active surface functional group content, and larger average pore diameter, thus showing improved performance, e.g., higher specific capacitance. According to the results of CRC measurements, the double-layer capacitance of CIMC-based K+-ISEs is up to 1000 µF. Besides, these SC-ISEs exhibit excellent potentiometric behaviors and long-term stability, reaching a low potential drift of 1.3 µV h−1 within 70 hours. Outstanding potential stability is also observed in the interference and water layer tests. As to the E0 reproducibility between electrodes, when Co2+/Co3+ redox couples are introduced into the ISM as internal reference species, CIMC-based SC-ISEs exhibit a very high reproducibility (SD) of ±0.7 mV (n = 6), compared with 7.3 mV for the counterparts without redox buffers and 32.2 mV for CWEs. In addition, CIMC can also be used as a solid-contact functional material to fabricate solid-contact REs (SC-REs) with low potential drift.351 Combining CIMC-based SC-ISEs and SC-REs, the same group further proposed a paper-based ion sensing platform (Fig. 7d and e).257 This disposable planar device can work without the need for any sensor pretreatments or reference electrolytes, having compatibility with mass-production printing techniques. Remarkably, recent advances in SC-REs also represent an important new trend for the development of REs to find more ideal alternatives to the common single and double liquid junction REs.352,353
In other research studies, Miller and coworkers evaluated the analytical performance of K+-ISEs based on two kinds of ordered porous carbon micro/nanomaterials (Fig. 7f and g).173 One ordered porous carbon micro/nanomaterial is a 3D porous carbon, i.e., the pyrolyzed carbon (PC) produced by interference lithography. The other is a porous GR (PG) analogue, which is converted from the PC after nickel coating and annealing. In this study, the authors found that the stability of PG-based SC-ISEs is worse compared to that of PC-based SC-ISEs, which may be attributed to the higher concentration of edge defects and oxygen functional groups of PG. Based on the integration of a PC-based K+-ISE and a microfluidic chip with a hollow microneedle, an ion-selective transdermal microneedle device was developed for the measurement of physiologically relevant concentrations. Later, the group of Bobacka developed a solid-contact Ag+-ISE using mesoporous carbon as the ion-to-electron transducer and used it to study the dissolution kinetics of Ag nanoparticles (AgNPs) in water.174 The influence of the morphology of mesoporous carbon (ordered mesoporous carbon (OMC) and disordered mesoporous carbon (DOMC)) on the electrochemical performance of the solid-contact layer was investigated by CV and EIS measurements, revealing that the OMC modified GC electrode has better capacitive characteristics than the DOMC modified one. The OMC-based Ag+-ISEs exhibit a stable potential response and an LOD of 1.58 × 10−7 M. In the investigation of the dissolution of AgNPs, these SC-ISEs achieved consistent results with inductively coupled plasma-mass spectrometry (ICP-MS).
Other porous carbon materials (e.g., microporous or nanoporous carbon rods) are used in a method similar to those reported for early porous graphite pellet substrates and are saturated with the membrane components to act as internal contacts that connect with the conductive substrate and the sensing membrane.354,355 The group of Chaniotakis prepared SC-ISEs based on a conductive porous carbon rod impregnated with a plasticized ionophore.356,357 The stable interfacial potential comes from the combination of the Ag wire and the internal Ag/AgCl reference element. These SC-ISEs can be used in a high-pressure environment or for continuous monitoring of remote water systems. They also developed a porous carbon-based Pb2+-ISE with an LOD of up to the picomolar level.358 It is observed that porous carbon contains no surface oxygen groups and has a large BET surface area. In addition, the performance characteristics of solid-contact K+-ISEs with several different structures based on polymeric hydrogels or nanoporous carbon were compared.359 In this study, nanoporous carbon-based SC-ISEs with a symmetric membrane configuration show a better performance than their counterparts with other structures. However, due to the hardness and brittleness of these materials, the size and design of SC-ISEs will be restricted. In another work, Weber and coworkers developed a microporous carbon (μPC)-based K+-ISE for measurements under high-pressure conditions where conventional liquid-contact ISEs failed.175 Prior to bonding with the PVC membrane, μPC is impregnated with the ionophore/additive cocktail to prevent premature sensor aging. The μPC-based K+-ISEs exhibit sufficient mechanical stability to withstand multiple pressurization cycles up to 105 bar, revealing their application potential for in situ deep-ocean analysis.


3.2.3 Carbon nanotubes. 
CNTs are one-dimensional carbon nanomaterials and can be classified into single-walled CNTs (SWCNTs) and multi-walled CNTs (MWCNTs) according to the number of layers of graphitic sheet coaxial tubes.360 Ever since their discovery in the late 20th century,361–363 CNTs have been extensively applied in the field of sensing technology because of their unique chemical, mechanical, optical, and electrical properties.364–367 The first introduction of SWCNTs as the ion-to-electron transducer in SC-ISEs was reported by the group of Rius in 2008,176 and then the implementation of MWCNTs was also demonstrated by the same group.181,182 These CNT-based SC-ISEs exhibit high potential stability and interference immunity due to the large specific surface area and excellent chemical stability of CNTs. The hydrophobicity of CNTs is beneficial to prevent the formation of a water layer at the back side of the ISM. Studies on the transduction mechanism of the SWCNT-based solid-contact layer using EIS and CV techniques have proved that a large double-layer capacitance can form at the interface between the SWCNT film and the electrolyte.127 Cuartero and coworkers further provided direct spectroscopic evidence for capacitive charging of the CNT-based solid-contact layer in SC-ISEs (Fig. 8a).146 These results suggest that the electrical transduction mechanism of CNT-based SC-ISEs is related to the formation of an electrical double layer and the capacitive mechanism induced by the surface adsorption of lipophilic anions onto the MWCNT-based solid-contact layer.
 Fig. 8  (a) Schematic diagram of a capacitive double layer created by the adsorption of either cations or anions onto the surface of CNTs (left). The synchrotron radiation-X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (SR-XPS) technique is used to perform the argon ion profile analysis of the MWCNT-based SC-ISE (right). Reproduced with permission.146 Copyright 2016, Royal Society of Chemistry. (b) The potentiometric response of MWCNT-based SC-ISEs to different anions (left). Comparison of the medium-term stability and light sensitivity between POT-based SC-ISEs and MWCNT-based SC-ISEs (right). Reproduced with permission.183 Copyright 2015, American Chemical Society. 
The early successful applications of CNTs in SC-ISEs have sparked significant interest for the development of novel CNT-based SC-ISEs, and to date, CNTs have become one of the most widely studied and applied nanomaterials in SC-ISEs. A lot of SC-ISEs with CNT-based ion-to-electron transducers have been developed for the detection of various target analytes, such as cations (K+,176,177 Na+,368,369 Ca2+,179,191,198 Pb2+,186,187,370–372 Cd2+,196 H+,181 Li+,195 Tl+,184 Ag+,196 and HN4+ (ref. 194)), anions (I−, NO2−, NO3−, CO32−, ClO4−, H2PO4−),178,182,183,185,197 choline and its derivatives,180 urea,373 polyquaterniums,374 pharmaceutical ingredients,188,189,375,376 anionic surfactants,190,377,378etc. Regarding the target analytes of SC-ISEs, reports on anion detection are far fewer than those on cation detection due to the partial difference in the transduction mechanism. In this regard, compared with CP-based SC-ISEs, the implementation of CNTs as the ion-to-electron transducer for SC-ISEs has its unique attractiveness in the detection of anions. The group of Bakker developed a general approach to prepare SC-ISEs for anion detection (Fig. 8b).183 Functionalized lipophilic MWCNTs are proved to be an effective ion-to-electron transducer that does not depend on the charge and nature of primary ions. In contrast, the POT-enabled transducer significantly exhibits a deficient performance, because its surface oxidation caused by the extraction of hydrophilic anions from the membrane may be energetically unfavorable when connected with an anion-selective membrane. Therefore, it is thought that CNT-enabled transducers based on the purely capacitive mechanism possess unique advantages in the field of anion detection and hence can be used as an attractive and general platform for the development of anion-selective sensors. Besides, the same group has recently proposed a direct potentiometric sensing method for the determination of anion concentration instead of activity with the use of MWCNT-based NO3−-ISEs.379 This approach successfully connects the ion concentration to equilibrium-type potentiometric ion sensors, avoiding the calculation of activity coefficients and liquid junction potentials, but not yet applicable to other divalent or multi-charged ions.
